<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Outline_of_communication" title="Outline of communication">Outline</a></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Communication" title="Template:Communication"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Communication" title="Template talk:Communication"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Communication&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Communication</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">communicare</i>, meaning "to share" or "to be in relation with")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> is usually defined as the transmission of <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>. The term can also refer just to the <a href="/wiki/Message" title="Message">message</a> communicated or to the field of inquiry studying such transmissions. There are many disagreements about its precise <a href="/wiki/Definition" title="Definition">definition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> John Peters argues that the difficulty of defining communication emerges from the fact that communication is both a <a href="/wiki/Universality_(philosophy)" title="Universality (philosophy)">universal</a> phenomenon (because everyone communicates) and a <a href="/wiki/Communication_studies" title="Communication studies">specific discipline</a> of institutional academic study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> One definitional strategy involves limiting what can be included in the category of communication (for example, requiring a "conscious intent" to persuade<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>). By this logic, one possible definition of communication is the act of developing <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">meaning</a> among <a href="/wiki/Subject_(philosophy)" title="Subject (philosophy)">entities</a> or <a href="/wiki/Organization" title="Organization">groups</a> through the use of sufficiently mutually understood <a href="/wiki/Sign" title="Sign">signs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbols</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Semiosis" title="Semiosis">semiotic</a> conventions.
</p><p>An important distinction is between verbal communication, which happens through the use of a language, and <a href="/wiki/Nonverbal_communication" title="Nonverbal communication">non-verbal communication</a>, for example, through gestures or facial expressions. Models of communication try to provide a detailed explanation of the different steps and entities involved. An influential model is given by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Shannon" title="Claude Shannon">Claude Shannon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warren_Weaver" title="Warren Weaver">Warren Weaver</a>, who argue that communicative <a href="/wiki/Motivation" title="Motivation">motivation</a> prompts the sender to compose a message, which is then encoded and transmitted. Once it has reached its destination, it is decoded and interpreted by the receiver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Communication is studied in various fields. <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">Information theory</a> investigates the quantification, storage, and communication of information in general. <a href="/wiki/Communication_studies" title="Communication studies">Communication studies</a> is concerned with human communication while the <a href="/wiki/Biocommunication_(science)" title="Biocommunication (science)">science of biocommunication</a> is interested in any form of communication between living organisms.
</p><p>Communication can be realized <a href="/wiki/Visual_communication" title="Visual communication">visually</a> (through images and <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">written</a> language), through <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">auditory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somatosensory_system" title="Somatosensory system">tactile</a>/<a href="/wiki/Haptic_communication" title="Haptic communication">haptic</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Braille" title="Braille">Braille</a> or other physical means), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfactory" title="Olfactory">olfactory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetic</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemical</a> means (or any combination thereof). Human communication is unique for its extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">abstract language</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Communication is usually understood as the transmission of information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHDCommunication_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHDCommunication-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CambridgeCommunication_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-CambridgeCommunication-13">[13]</a></sup> In this regard, a <a href="/wiki/Message" title="Message">message</a> is conveyed from a sender to a receiver using some form of medium, such as sound, paper, bodily movements, or electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosengren2000_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosengren2000-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munodawafa2008_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Munodawafa2008-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackburn1996b_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blackburn1996b-16">[16]</a></sup> In a different sense, the term "communication" can also refer just to the message that is being communicated or to the <a href="/wiki/Communication_studies" title="Communication studies">field of inquiry studying such transmissions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHDCommunication_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHDCommunication-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CambridgeCommunication_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-CambridgeCommunication-13">[13]</a></sup> There is a lot of disagreement concerning the precise characterization of communication and various scholars have raised doubts that any single definition can capture the term accurately. These difficulties come from the fact that the term is applied to diverse phenomena in different contexts, often with slightly different meanings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dance1970_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dance1970-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon2022_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon2022-18">[18]</a></sup> Despite these problems, the question of the right definition is of great theoretical importance since it affects the research process on all levels. This includes issues like which <a href="/wiki/Empirical_evidence" title="Empirical evidence">empirical phenomena</a> are observed, how they are categorized, which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypotheses" title="Hypotheses">hypotheses</a> and laws are formulated as well as how systematic theories based on these steps are articulated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dance1970_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dance1970-17">[17]</a></sup> The word "communication" has its root in the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> verb "communicare", which means "to share" or "to make common".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosengren2000_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rosengren2000-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some theorists give very broad definitions of communication that encompass unconscious and non-human behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dance1970_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dance1970-17">[17]</a></sup> In this regard, many animals communicate within their own species and even plants like flowers may be said to communicate by attracting bees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosengren2000_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rosengren2000-14">[14]</a></sup> Other researchers restrict communication to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conscious" title="Conscious">conscious</a> interactions among human beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dance1970_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dance1970-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosengren2000_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rosengren2000-14">[14]</a></sup> Some definitions focus on the use of symbols and signs while others emphasize the role of understanding, interaction, power, or transmission of ideas. Various characterizations see the communicator's <a href="/wiki/Intention" title="Intention">intent</a> to send a message as a central component. On this view, the transmission of information is not sufficient for communication if it happens unintentionally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dance1970_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Dance1970-17">[17]</a></sup> An important version of this view is given by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Grice" title="Paul Grice">Paul Grice</a>, who identifies communication with <a href="/wiki/Action_(philosophy)" title="Action (philosophy)">actions</a> that aim to make the recipient aware of the communicator's intention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackburn1996_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blackburn1996-19">[19]</a></sup> One question in this regard is whether only the successful transmission of information should be regarded as communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dance1970_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Dance1970-17">[17]</a></sup> For example, distortion may interfere and change the actual message from what was originally intended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munodawafa2008_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Munodawafa2008-15">[15]</a></sup> A closely related problem is whether acts of deliberate <a href="/wiki/Deception" title="Deception">deception</a> constitute communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dance1970_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-Dance1970-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to an influential and broad definition by <a href="/wiki/I._A._Richards" title="I. A. Richards">I. A. Richards</a>, communication happens when one <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> acts upon its environment in order to transmit its own <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> to another mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon2022_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon2022-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Another important characterization is due to <a href="/wiki/Claude_Shannon" title="Claude Shannon">Claude Shannon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warren_Weaver" title="Warren Weaver">Warren Weaver</a>. On their view, communication involves the interaction of several components, such as a source, a message, an encoder, a channel, a decoder, and a receiver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon2022_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon2022-18">[18]</a></sup> The paradigmatic form of communication happens between two or several individuals. However, it can also take place on a larger level, for example, between organizations, social classes, or nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosengren2000_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-Rosengren2000-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Niklas_Luhmann" title="Niklas Luhmann">Niklas Luhmann</a> rejects the view that communication is, on its most fundamental level, an interaction between two distinct parties. Instead, he holds that "only communication can communicate" and tries to provide a conceptualization in terms of <a href="/wiki/Systems_theory" title="Systems theory">autopoietic systems</a> without any reference to consciousness or life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luhmann1992_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luhmann1992-21">[21]</a></sup> John Peters sees communication as "an apparent answer to the painful divisions between <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">self</a> and other, private and public, and inner thought and outer world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Verbal_communication">Verbal communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Verbal communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">Linguistics</a></div>
<p>Verbal communication is the spoken or written conveyance of a message. Human <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> can be defined as a <a href="/wiki/System" title="System">system</a> of <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbols</a> (also known as <a href="/wiki/Lexeme" title="Lexeme">lexemes</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammars</a> (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/rule" title="wikt:rule">rules</a>) by which the symbols are manipulated. The word "language" also refers to common properties of languages. <a href="/wiki/Language_acquisition" title="Language acquisition">Language learning</a> normally occurs most intensively during human childhood. Most of the large number of human languages use patterns of <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">sound</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gesture" title="Gesture">gesture</a> for symbols which enable communication with others around them. Languages tend to share certain properties, although there are exceptions. <a href="/wiki/Constructed_language" title="Constructed language">Constructed languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Esperanto" title="Esperanto">Esperanto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">programming languages</a>, and various mathematical formalisms are not necessarily restricted to the properties shared by human languages.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Communicators' diverse efforts to produce and interpret meaning in language are functionally constrained by that language's prototypical <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a> (sounds that typically appear in a language), <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> (what counts as a word), <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a> (word-order), <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantics</a> (conventional meaning of words), and <a href="/wiki/Pragmatics" title="Pragmatics">pragmatics</a> (which meanings are conventional to which contexts).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The meanings that are attached to words can be literal, or otherwise known as denotative; relating to the topic being discussed, or, the meanings take context and relationships into account, otherwise known as connotative; relating to the feelings, history, and power dynamics of the communicators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contrary to popular belief, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signed_languages" title="Signed languages">signed languages</a> of the world (e.g., <a href="/wiki/American_Sign_Language" title="American Sign Language">American Sign Language</a>) are considered to be verbal communication because their sign vocabulary, grammar, and other linguistic structures abide by all the necessary classifications as spoken languages. There are however, nonverbal elements to signed languages, such as the speed, intensity, and size of signs that are made. A signer might sign "yes" in response to a question, or they might sign a sarcastic-large slow yes to convey a different nonverbal meaning. The sign yes is the verbal message while the other movements add nonverbal meaning to the message.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Written_form">Written form</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Written form">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Over time the forms of and ideas about communication have evolved through the continuing progression of technology. Advances include communications psychology and <a href="/wiki/Media_psychology" title="Media psychology">media psychology</a>, an emerging field of study.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The progression of written communication can be divided into three "information communication revolutions":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Written communication first emerged through the use of pictographs. The <a href="/wiki/Pictogram" title="Pictogram">pictograms</a> were made in stone, hence written communication was not yet mobile. Pictograms began to develop standardized and simplified forms.</li>
<li>The next step occurred when writing began to appear on <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a>, papyrus, clay, wax, and other media with commonly shared <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing systems</a>. Communication became mobile.</li>
<li>The final stage is characterized by the transfer of information through controlled waves of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> (i.e., radio, microwave, infrared) and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signal_(electrical_engineering)" title="Signal (electrical engineering)">electronic signals</a>.</li></ol>
<p>Communication is thus a process by which meaning is assigned and conveyed in an attempt to create shared understanding. Gregory Bateson called it "the replication of tautologies in the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This process, which requires a vast repertoire of skills in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interpersonal" title="Interpersonal">interpersonal</a> processing, listening, observing, speaking, questioning, analyzing, gestures, and evaluating enables <a href="/wiki/Collaboration" title="Collaboration">collaboration</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cooperation" title="Cooperation">cooperation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (February 2021)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-verbal_communication">Non-verbal communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Non-verbal communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nonverbal_communication" title="Nonverbal communication">Nonverbal communication</a></div>
<p>Nonverbal communication explains the processes that convey a type of information in a form of non-linguistic representations. Examples of nonverbal communication include <a href="/wiki/Haptic_communication" title="Haptic communication">haptic communication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chronemics" title="Chronemics">chronemic communication</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gestures" title="Gestures">gestures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Body_language" title="Body language">body language</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Facial_expressions" title="Facial expressions">facial expressions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eye_contact" title="Eye contact">eye contact</a> etc. Nonverbal communication also relates to the intent of a message. Examples of intent are voluntary, intentional movements like shaking a hand or winking, as well as involuntary, such as sweating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Speech also contains nonverbal elements known as <a href="/wiki/Paralanguage" title="Paralanguage">paralanguage</a>, e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhythm_(linguistics)" title="Rhythm (linguistics)">rhythm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intonation_(linguistics)" title="Intonation (linguistics)">intonation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tempo_of_speech" title="Tempo of speech">tempo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress</a>. It affects communication most at the subconscious level and establishes trust. Likewise, written texts include nonverbal elements such as handwriting style, the spatial arrangement of words and the use of <a href="/wiki/Emoticon" title="Emoticon">emoticons</a> to convey emotion.
</p><p>Nonverbal communication demonstrates one of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Watzlawick" title="Paul Watzlawick">Paul Watzlawick's</a> laws: you cannot not communicate. Once proximity has formed awareness, living creatures begin interpreting any signals received.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Some of the functions of nonverbal communication in humans are to complement and illustrate, to reinforce and emphasize, to replace and substitute, to control and regulate, and to contradict the denotative message.
</p><p>Nonverbal cues are heavily relied on to express communication and to interpret others' communication and can replace or substitute verbal messages.
</p><p>There are several reasons as to why non-verbal communication plays a vital role in communication:
</p>
<ul><li>"Non-verbal communication is omnipresent."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> They are included in every single communication act. To have total communication, all non-verbal channels such as the body, face, voice, appearance, touch, distance, timing, and other environmental forces must be engaged during face-to-face interaction. Written communication can also have non-verbal attributes. E-mails, web chats, and the <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a> have options to change text font colours, stationery, add emoticons, capitalization, and pictures in order to capture non-verbal cues into a verbal medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Non-verbal behaviours are multifunctional."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgoon_et_al.,_p._4_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burgoon_et_al.,_p._4-35">[35]</a></sup> Many different non-verbal channels are engaged at the same time in communication acts and allow the chance for simultaneous messages to be sent and received.</li>
<li>"Non-verbal behaviours may form a universal language system."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgoon_et_al.,_p._4_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burgoon_et_al.,_p._4-35">[35]</a></sup> Smiling, crying, pointing, caressing, and glaring are non-verbal behaviours that are used and understood by people regardless of nationality. Such non-verbal signals allow the most basic form of communication when verbal communication is not effective due to language barriers.</li></ul>
<p>When verbal messages contradict non-verbal messages, observation of non-verbal behaviour is relied on to judge another's attitudes and feelings, rather than assuming the truth of the verbal message alone.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Non verbal communication can take the following forms:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paralinguistics" title="Paralinguistics">Paralinguistics</a> are the elements other than language where the voice is involved in communication and includes <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(music)" title="Pitch (music)">pitch</a>, vocal cues etc. It also includes sounds from throat and all these are greatly influenced by cultural differences across borders.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Proxemics" title="Proxemics">Proxemics</a> deals with the concept of the <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> element in communication. Proxemics explains four zones of spaces, namely intimate, personal, social and public. This concept differs from culture to culture as the permissible space varies in different countries.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artifactics" title="Artifactics">Artifactics</a> studies the <a href="/wiki/Nonverbal_communication" title="Nonverbal communication">non verbal signals</a> or communication which emerges from personal accessories such as the dress or fashion accessories worn and it varies with culture as people of different countries follow different dress codes.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chronemics" title="Chronemics">Chronemics</a> deals with the time aspects of communication and also includes the importance given to time. Some issues explaining this concept are pauses, silences and response lag during an <a href="/wiki/Interaction" title="Interaction">interaction</a>. This aspect of communication is also influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_difference" title="Cultural difference">cultural differences</a> as it is well known that there is a great difference in the value given by different cultures to time.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kinesics" title="Kinesics">Kinesics</a> mainly deals with <a href="/wiki/Body_language" title="Body language">body language</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Neutral_spine" title="Neutral spine">postures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gesture" title="Gesture">gestures</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Head_nod" title="Head nod">head nods</a>, leg movements, etc. In different countries, the same gestures and postures are used to convey different messages. Sometimes even a particular kinesic indicating something good in a country may have a negative meaning in another culture.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication_models">Communication models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Communication models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Models_of_communication" title="Models of communication">Models of communication</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lasswells_Model_of_Communication">Lasswells Model of Communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Lasswells Model of Communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Harold_Lasswell" title="Harold Lasswell">Harold Lasswell</a> is considered to be one of the primary founders of communication theories and he helped communications become a respected and legitimate study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> He developed the Lasswell Model of communication of communication during his professorship at Yale. It is now used as an umbrella term for other models of communication due to its simplicities to allow for multiple and very different interpretations to theorize with. It was published in "The Structure and Function of Communication in Society" essay in 1948. Lasswell was thinking about mass media and the role radio played in the 1930s. It was extremely popular after the second world war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lasswell_388–396_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lasswell_388–396-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Lasswell model is broken into five parts-the five W's, it focuses on the "who", "what", "whom", "which Channel" and "what effect". Lasswell brings up that there is three functions of communication, there is surveillance of the environment, correlation of components of society and cultural transmission between generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Who, Communication is not only one person, it is newspapers, websites, television stations and radio stations. This communication is developed by people who run this organized institutions, reporters, editors etc.</li></ul>
<ul><li>Says What, refers to analyzing and identifying the content that was given.</li></ul>
<ul><li>To Whom, who is the audience and how does the audience receive this information,</li></ul>
<ul><li>Which Channel, refers to the media and how it is going to analyzed. The interactivity of Media</li></ul>
<ul><li>What effect, refers to what is taken from this piece of information</li></ul>
<p>There are advantages as well as some critiques mentioned about the Lasswell Model. Some of the advantages are that the concept is easy and simple, it suits most types of communication and its the main concept of effect.
</p><p>Some scholars believe that the Lasswell model of communication is no longer relevant, although at the time it was extremely significant. The critiques that have been brought up for the Lasswell model is the concept of feedback not mention, and the concept of noise is not mentioned. It was there where other theories started to come out to make up for the Lasswells method not having these factors in it, specially the Shannon and Weaver model. Another critique was that the Lasswell model is a linear model, it goes against other types of communications that show that it is more of a back and forth experience. It also does not mention power in the communication process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Power was highlighted specially in Sturat Halls methods and concepts. Hall took parts from the Lasswell method and started reworking it to fit the idea of power in media, who has power and what message are they putting out. Hall examines how we view organizations and mass media and how they enforce certain meanings to appease their agenda.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shannon_and_Weaver_Model">Shannon and Weaver Model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Shannon and Weaver Model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Communication_shannon-weaver2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Communication_shannon-weaver2.svg/220px-Communication_shannon-weaver2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Communication_shannon-weaver2.svg/330px-Communication_shannon-weaver2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Communication_shannon-weaver2.svg/440px-Communication_shannon-weaver2.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Communication_shannon-weaver2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shannon and Weaver Model of Communication</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Communication_emisor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="351" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Communication_emisor.jpg/220px-Communication_emisor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Communication_emisor.jpg/330px-Communication_emisor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b0/Communication_emisor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Communication_emisor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Communication major dimensions scheme</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Interaction_comm_model.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Interaction_comm_model.svg/220px-Interaction_comm_model.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Interaction_comm_model.svg/330px-Interaction_comm_model.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Interaction_comm_model.svg/440px-Interaction_comm_model.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Interaction_comm_model.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interactional Model of Communication</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Smcr.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="933" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Smcr.jpg/220px-Smcr.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Smcr.jpg/330px-Smcr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Smcr.jpg/440px-Smcr.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Smcr.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Berlo's Sender-Message-Channel-Receiver Model of Communication</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Transactional_comm_model.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="740" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Transactional_comm_model.jpg/220px-Transactional_comm_model.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Transactional_comm_model.jpg/330px-Transactional_comm_model.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Transactional_comm_model.jpg/440px-Transactional_comm_model.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Transactional_comm_model.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Transactional model of communication</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Encoding_communication.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="345" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Encoding_communication.jpg/220px-Encoding_communication.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Encoding_communication.jpg/330px-Encoding_communication.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/Encoding_communication.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Encoding_communication.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Communication code scheme</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linear_comm_model.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="135" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Linear_comm_model.svg/220px-Linear_comm_model.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Linear_comm_model.svg/330px-Linear_comm_model.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Linear_comm_model.svg/440px-Linear_comm_model.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linear_comm_model.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Linear Communication Model</div></div></div>
<p>The first major model for communication was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Shannon" title="Claude Shannon">Claude Shannon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warren_Weaver" title="Warren Weaver">Warren Weaver</a> for Bell Laboratories in 1949<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The original model was designed to mirror the functioning of radio and telephone technologies. Their initial model consisted of three primary parts: sender, channel, and receiver. The sender was the part of a telephone a person spoke into, the channel was the telephone itself, and the receiver was the part of the phone where one could hear the other person. Shannon and Weaver also recognized that often there is static that interferes with one listening to a telephone <a href="/wiki/Conversation" title="Conversation">conversation</a>, which they deemed noise.
</p><p>In a simple model, often referred to as the transmission model or standard view of communication, information or content (e.g. a message in <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">natural language</a>) is sent in some form (as <a href="/wiki/Spoken_language" title="Spoken language">spoken language</a>) from an emitter (<i>emisor</i> in the picture)/sender/encoder to a destination/receiver/decoder. This common conception of communication simply views communication as a means of sending and receiving information. The strengths of this model are simplicity, generality, and quantifiability. Claude Shannon and Warren Weaver structured this model based on the following elements:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The formation of communicative <a href="/wiki/Motivation" title="Motivation">motivation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Message" title="Message">Message</a> composition (further <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">internal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technical</a> elaboration on what exactly to express).</li>
<li>Message encoding (for example, into <a href="/wiki/Digital_data" title="Digital data">digital data</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Written_text" title="Written text">written text</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spoken_word" title="Spoken word">speech</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picture" title="Picture">pictures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gesture" title="Gesture">gestures</a> and so on).</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transmission_(telecommunications)" title="Transmission (telecommunications)">Transmission</a> of the encoded message as a sequence of signals using a specific <a href="/wiki/Communication_channel" title="Communication channel">channel</a> or <a href="/wiki/Media_(communication)" title="Media (communication)">medium</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Communication_noise" title="Communication noise">Noise sources</a> such as natural forces and in some cases human activity (both intentional and accidental) begin influencing the quality of signals propagating from the sender to one or more receivers.</li>
<li><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/reception" title="wikt:reception">Reception</a> of signals and reassembling of the encoded message from a sequence of received signals.</li>
<li>Decoding of the reassembled encoded message.</li>
<li><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/interpretation" title="wikt:interpretation">Interpretation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nous" title="Nous">making sense</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presumed" title="Presumed">presumed</a> original message.</li></ol>
<p>These elements are now understood to be substantially overlapping and recursive activities rather than steps in a sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> For example, communicative actions can start before a communicator formulates a conscious attempt to do so,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> as in the case of <a href="/wiki/Phatic_expression" title="Phatic expression">phatics</a>; likewise, communicators modify their intentions and formulations of a message in response to real-time feedback (e.g., a change in <a href="/wiki/Facial_expression" title="Facial expression">facial expression</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-44">[44]</a></sup> Practices of decoding and interpretation are culturally enacted, not just by individuals (<a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genre</a> conventions, for instance, trigger anticipatory expectations for how a message is to be received), and receivers of any message operationalize their own frames of reference in interpretation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shannon and Weaver argued that there were three levels of problems for communication within this theory.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The technical problem: how accurately can the message be transmitted?</li>
<li>The semantic problem: how precisely is the meaning conveyed?</li>
<li>The effectiveness problem: how effectively does the received meaning affect behavior?</li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Daniel_Chandler" title="Daniel Chandler">Daniel Chandler</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> critiques the transmission model by stating:
</p>
<ul><li>It assumes communicators are isolated individuals.</li>
<li>No allowance for differing purposes.</li>
<li>No allowance for differing interpretations.</li>
<li>No allowance for unequal power relations.</li>
<li>No allowance for situational contexts.</li></ul>
<p>In 1960, David Berlo expanded on Shannon and Weaver's (1949) linear model of communication and created the SMCR Model of Communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The Sender-Message-Channel-Receiver Model of communication separated the model into clear parts and has been expanded upon by other scholars.
</p><p>Communication is usually described along a few major dimensions: message (what type of things are communicated), source/emisor/sender/encoder (from whom), form (in which form), channel (through which <a href="/wiki/Media_(communication)" title="Media (communication)">medium</a>), destination/receiver/target/decoder (to whom). Wilbur Schram (1954) also indicated that we should also examine the impact that a message has (both desired and undesired) on the target of the message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Between parties, communication includes acts that confer knowledge and experiences, give advice and commands, and ask questions. These acts may take many forms, in one of the various manners of communication. The form depends on the abilities of the group communicating. Together, communication content and form make <a href="/wiki/Message" title="Message">messages</a> that are sent towards a destination. The target can be oneself, another <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interpersonal_Communication" title="Interpersonal Communication">person</a> or being, another entity (such as a corporation or group of beings).
</p><p>Communication can be seen as processes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Information_transmission" title="Information transmission">information transmission</a> with three levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiotic" title="Semiotic">semiotic</a> rules:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Pragmatics" title="Pragmatics">Pragmatic</a> (concerned with the relations between signs/expressions and their users).</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semantic" title="Semantic">Semantic</a> (study of relationships between signs and symbols and what they represent).</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syntactic" title="Syntactic">Syntactic</a> (formal properties of signs and symbols).</li></ol>
<p>Therefore, communication is <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_relationship" title="Interpersonal relationship">social interaction</a> where at least two interacting agents share a common set of signs and a common set of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiotic" title="Semiotic">semiotic</a> rules. This commonly held rule in some sense ignores <a href="/wiki/Semiotics" title="Semiotics">autocommunication</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Intrapersonal_communication" title="Intrapersonal communication">intrapersonal communication</a> via <a href="/wiki/Diary" title="Diary">diaries</a> or self-talk, both secondary phenomena that followed the primary acquisition of communicative competences within social interactions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In light of these weaknesses, Barnlund (2008) proposed a transactional model of communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The basic premise of the transactional model of communication is that individuals are simultaneously engaging in the sending and receiving of messages.
</p><p>In a slightly more complex form a sender and a receiver are linked <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/reciprocal" title="wikt:reciprocal">reciprocally</a>. This second attitude of communication, referred to as the constitutive model or constructionist view, focuses on how an individual communicates as the determining factor of the way the message will be interpreted. Communication is viewed as a conduit; a passage in which information travels from one individual to another and this information becomes separate from the communication itself. A particular instance of communication is called a <a href="/wiki/Speech_act" title="Speech act">speech act</a>. The sender's personal filters and the receiver's personal filters may vary depending upon different regional traditions, cultures, or gender; which may alter the intended meaning of message contents. In the presence of "<a href="/wiki/Noise" title="Noise">communication noise</a>" on the transmission channel (air, in this case), reception and decoding of content may be faulty, and thus the speech act may not achieve the desired effect. One problem with this encode-transmit-receive-decode model is that the processes of encoding and decoding imply that the sender and receiver each possess something that functions as a <a href="/wiki/Codebook" title="Codebook">codebook</a>, and that these two code books are, at the very least, similar if not identical. Although something like code books is implied by the model, they are nowhere represented in the model, which creates many conceptual difficulties.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Theories of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coregulation" title="Coregulation">coregulation</a> describe communication as a creative and dynamic continuous process, rather than a discrete exchange of information. Canadian media scholar <a href="/wiki/Harold_Innis" title="Harold Innis">Harold Innis</a> had the theory that people use different types of media to communicate and which one they choose to use will offer different possibilities for the shape and durability of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wark1997_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wark1997-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2017)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> His famous example of this is using <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a> and looking at the ways they built themselves out of media with very different properties stone and papyrus. Papyrus is what he called '<a href="/wiki/General_semantics" title="General semantics">Space Binding</a>'. it made possible the transmission of written orders across space, empires and enables the waging of distant military campaigns and colonial administration. The other is stone and '<a href="/wiki/General_semantics" title="General semantics">Time Binding</a>', through the construction of temples and the pyramids can sustain their authority generation to generation, through this media they can change and shape communication in their society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wark1997_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wark1997-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2017)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="As_an_academic_discipline">As an academic discipline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: As an academic discipline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Communication_studies" title="Communication studies">Communication studies</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Communication_theory" title="Communication theory">Communication theory</a></div>
<p>The academic discipline that deals with processes of human communication is communication studies. The discipline encompasses a range of topics, from face-to-face conversation to mass media outlets such as television broadcasting. Communication studies also examines how messages are interpreted through the political, cultural, economic, semiotic, hermeneutic, and social dimensions of their contexts. <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">Statistics</a>, as a quantitative approach to communication science, has also been incorporated into research on communication science in order to help substantiate claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organizational_communication">Organizational communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Organizational communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organizational_communication" title="Organizational communication">Organizational communication</a></div>
<p>Business communication is used for a wide variety of activities including, but not limited to: strategic communications planning, media relations, internal communications, <a href="/wiki/Public_relations" title="Public relations">public relations</a> (which can include social media, broadcast and written communications, and more), brand management, <a href="/wiki/Reputation_management" title="Reputation management">reputation management</a>, speech-writing, customer-client relations, and internal/employee communications.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Companies with limited resources may choose to engage in only a few of these activities, while larger organizations may employ a full spectrum of communications. Since it is relatively difficult to develop such a broad range of skills, communications professionals often specialize in one or two of these areas but usually have at least a working knowledge of most of them. By far, the most important qualifications communications professionals must possess are excellent writing ability, good 'people' skills, and the capacity to think critically and strategically.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Business communication could also refer to the style of communication within a given corporate entity (i.e. email conversation styles, or internal communication styles).
</p><p>The Classical Approach:
</p><p>The classical approach comes from the management theory by Frederick Taylor who was the founder of the scientific management theory as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-52">[52]</a></sup> The main idea of the classical approach of organizational communication is that the theory compares organizations to a machine. The theory observed and analyze that workers perform the task they are given to in order to contribute to the overall well-being of the organization. Each member has their purpose in the group, just like a part of a machine works does its tasks while cooperate with other parts to have a well-managed, functioning machine. Additionally, just like a machine that collapse when one part fails to function. An organization will fall apart when members are not doing their designated task appropriately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Human Relation Approach:
</p><p>The human relation approach is based from several different theorists such as: Elton Mayo, McGregors's Douglas, Abraham Maslow, Mary Parker Follett's and Argyris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-52">[52]</a></sup> The main idea of the human relation approach of organizational communication is that the theory compares organizations to a family. As this theory compares organization to a family, it focuses on workers satisfaction and the relationship within the organizations more compared to the work performance element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The human relation approach emphasizes the importance of employee attitudes, and encourage organizations management team to focus on interpersonal relationships, group dynamics, and leadership styles in achieving organizational effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_communication">Political communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Political communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Communication is one of the most relevant tools in political strategies, including persuasion and <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">mass media</a> research and online media research, the effort of the strategist is that of getting a precise decoding, avoiding "message reactance", that is, message refusal. The reaction to a message is referred also in terms of approach to a message, as follows:
</p>
<ul><li>In "radical reading" the audience rejects the meanings, values, and viewpoints built into the text by its makers. Effect: message refusal.</li>
<li>In "dominant reading", the audience accepts the meanings, values, and viewpoints built into the text by its makers. Effect: message acceptance.</li>
<li>In "subordinate reading" the audience accepts, by and large, the meanings, values, and worldview built into the text by its makers. Effect: obey to the message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Holistic approaches are used by communication campaign leaders and communication strategists in order to examine all the options, "actors" and channels that can generate change in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiotic" title="Semiotic">semiotic</a> landscape, that is, change in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perceptions" title="Perceptions">perceptions</a>, change in <a href="/wiki/Credibility" title="Credibility">credibility</a>, change in the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memetic" title="Memetic">memetic</a> background", change in the image of movements, of candidates, players and managers as perceived by key influencers that can have a role in generating the desired "end-state".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The modern political communication field is highly influenced by the framework and practices of "information operations" doctrines that derive their nature from strategic and military studies. According to this view, what is really relevant is the concept of acting on the Information Environment. The information environment is the aggregate of individuals, organizations, and systems that collect, process, disseminate, or act on information. This environment consists of three interrelated dimensions, which continuously interact with individuals, organizations, and systems. These dimensions are known as physical, informational, and cognitive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpersonal_communication">Interpersonal communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Interpersonal communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_communication" title="Interpersonal communication">Interpersonal communication</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Copy_edit plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Copy_edit" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="45" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Acap.svg/36px-Acap.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Acap.svg/54px-Acap.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Acap.svg/72px-Acap.svg.png 2x" width="36"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may require <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Basic_copyediting" title="Wikipedia:Basic copyediting">copy editing</a> for grammar, style, cohesion, tone, or spelling</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> You can assist by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit">editing it</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2022</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In simple terms, <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_communication" title="Interpersonal communication">interpersonal communication</a> is the communication between one person and another (or others). It is often referred to as face-to-face communication between two (or more) people. Both verbal and nonverbal communication, or <a href="/wiki/Body_language" title="Body language">body language</a>, play a part in how one person understands another, and attribute to one's own <a href="/wiki/Soft_skills" title="Soft skills">soft skills</a>. In verbal interpersonal communication there are two types of messages being sent: a content message and a relational message. Content messages are messages about the topic at hand and relational messages are messages about the relationship itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-58">[58]</a></sup> This means that relational messages come across in <i>how</i> one says something and it demonstrates a person's feelings, whether positive or negative, towards the individual they are talking to, indicating not only how they feel about the topic at hand, but also how they feel about their relationship with the other individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are many different aspects of <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_communication" title="Interpersonal communication">interpersonal communication</a> including:
</p>
<ul><li>Audiovisual Perception of Communication Problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The concept follows the idea that our words change what form they take based on the stress level or urgency of the situation. It also explores the concept that stuttering during speech shows the audience that there is a problem or that the situation is more stressful.</li>
<li>The Attachment Theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> This is the combined work of John Bowlby and Mary Ainsworth (Ainsworth &amp; Bowlby, 1991) This theory follows the relationships that builds between a mother and child, and the impact it has on their relationships with others.</li>
<li>Emotional Intelligence and Triggers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Emotional Intelligence focuses on the ability to monitor ones own emotions as well as those of others. Emotional Triggers focus on events or people that tend to set off intense, emotional reactions within individuals.</li>
<li>Attribution Theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> This is the study of how individuals explain what causes different events and behaviors.</li>
<li>The Power of Words (Verbal communications).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Verbal communication focuses heavily on the power of words, and how those words are said. It takes into consideration tone, volume, and choice of words.</li>
<li>Nonverbal Communication. It focuses heavily on the setting that the words are conveyed in, as well as the physical tone of the words.</li>
<li>Ethics in Personal Relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> It is about a space of mutual responsibility between two individuals, it's about giving and receiving in a relationship. This theory is explored by Dawn J. Lipthrott in the article What IS Relationship? What is Ethical Partnership?</li>
<li>Deception in Communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> This concept goes into that everyone lies, and how this can impact relationships. This theory is explored by James Hearn in his article Interpersonal Deception Theory: Ten Lessons for Negotiators.</li>
<li>Conflict in Couples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> This focuses on the impact that social media has on relationships, as well as how to communicate through conflict. This theory is explored by Amanda Lenhart and Maeve Duggan in their paper Couples, the Internet, and Social Media.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_communication">Family communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Family communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Family_communication&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Family communication (page does not exist)">Family communication</a> is the study of the communication perspective in a broadly defined family, with intimacy and trusting relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The main goal of family communication is to understand the interactions of family and the pattern of behaviors of family members in different circumstances. Open and honest communication creates an atmosphere that allows family members to express their differences as well as love and admiration for one another. It also helps to understand the feelings of one another.
</p><p>Family communication study looks at topics such as family rules, family roles or family dialectics and how those factors could affect the communication between family members. Researchers develop theories to understand communication behaviors. Family communication study also digs deep into certain time periods of family life such as marriage, parenthood or divorce and how communication stands in those situations. It is important for family members to understand communication as a trusted way which leads to a well constructed family.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rhetoric">Rhetoric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Rhetoric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to scholar Anne Beaufort, communication is also interested in rhetoric as a method of investigating "oral and written communications, particularly with regard to the desired effect on an audience, and lately, with visual communications as well."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Barriers_to_effectiveness">Barriers to effectiveness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Barriers to effectiveness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Barriers to effective communication can distort the message or intention of the message being conveyed. This may result in failure of the communication process or cause an effect that is undesirable. These include filtering, <a href="/wiki/Selective_perception" title="Selective perception">selective perception</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information_overload" title="Information overload">information overload</a>, emotions, language, silence, <a href="/wiki/Communication_apprehension" title="Communication apprehension">communication apprehension</a>, gender differences and <a href="/wiki/Political_correctness" title="Political correctness">political correctness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Noise">Noise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Noise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In any communication model, noise is interference with the decoding of messages sent over the channel by an encoder. To face communication noise, redundancy and acknowledgement must often be used. Acknowledgements are messages from the addressee informing the originator that his/her communication has been received and is understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Message repetition and feedback about message received are necessary in the presence of noise to reduce the probability of misunderstanding.
</p><p>The act of disambiguation regards the attempt of reducing noise and wrong interpretations, when the semantic value or meaning of a sign can be subject to noise, or in presence of multiple meanings, which makes the sense-making difficult. Disambiguation attempts to decrease the likelihood of misunderstanding. This is also a fundamental skill in communication processes activated by counselors, psychotherapists, interpreters, and in coaching sessions based on colloquium. In Information Technology, the disambiguation process and the automatic disambiguation of meanings of words and sentences has also been an interest and concern since the earliest days of computer treatment of language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_aspects">Cultural aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cultural aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cultural differences exist within countries (tribal/regional differences, dialects and so on), between religious groups and in organisations or at an organisational level – where companies, teams and units may have different expectations, norms and idiolects. Families and family groups may also experience the effect of cultural barriers to communication within and between different family members or groups. For example: words, colours and symbols have different meanings in different cultures. In most parts of the world, nodding your head means agreement, shaking your head means "no", but this is not true everywhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Communication to a great extent is influenced by culture and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_variations_in_adoption" title="Cultural variations in adoption">cultural variables</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Understanding <i>cultural aspects of communication</i> refers to having knowledge of different cultures in order to communicate effectively with cross culture people. Cultural aspects of communication are of great relevance in today's world which is now a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_village_(term)" title="Global village (term)">global village</a>, thanks to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Globalisation" title="Globalisation">globalisation</a>. Cultural aspects of communication are the cultural differences which influence communication across borders. So in order to have an effective communication across the world it is desirable to have a knowledge of cultural variables effecting communication.
</p><p>According to Michael Walsh and <a href="/wiki/Ghil%27ad_Zuckermann" title="Ghil'ad Zuckermann">Ghil'ad Zuckermann</a>, Western conversational interaction is typically "dyadic", between two particular people, where eye contact is important and the speaker controls the interaction; and "contained" in a relatively short, defined time frame. However, traditional Aboriginal conversational interaction is "communal", broadcast to many people, eye contact is not important, the listener controls the interaction; and "continuous", spread over a longer, indefinite time frame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engaging_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Engaging-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nonhuman">Nonhuman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Nonhuman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Biocommunication_(science)" title="Biocommunication (science)">Biocommunication (science)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interspecies_communication" title="Interspecies communication">Interspecies communication</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biosemiotics" title="Biosemiotics">Biosemiotics</a></div>
<p>Every <a href="/wiki/Information_exchange" title="Information exchange">information exchange</a> between living organisms — i.e. transmission of <a href="/wiki/Signalling_theory" title="Signalling theory">signals</a> that involve a living sender and <a href="/wiki/Receiver_(information_theory)" title="Receiver (information theory)">receiver</a> can be considered a form of communication; and even primitive creatures such as corals are competent to communicate. Nonhuman communication also include <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">cell signaling</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cellular_communication_(biology)" title="Cellular communication (biology)">cellular communication</a>, and chemical transmissions between primitive organisms like <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and within the <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungal</a> kingdoms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
