<p>The broad field of <a href="/wiki/Animal_communication" title="Animal communication">animal communication</a> encompasses most of the issues in <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">ethology</a>. <a href="/wiki/Animal_communication" title="Animal communication">Animal communication</a> can be defined as any <a href="/wiki/Behavior" title="Behavior">behavior</a> of one animal that affects the current or future behavior of another animal. The study of animal communication, called <i>zoo semiotics</i> (distinguishable from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthroposemiotics" title="Anthroposemiotics">anthroposemiotics</a>, the study of human communication) has played an important part in the development of <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">ethology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sociobiology" title="Sociobiology">sociobiology</a>, and the study of <a href="/wiki/Animal_cognition" title="Animal cognition">animal cognition</a>. Animal communication, and indeed the understanding of the animal world in general, is a rapidly growing field, and even in the 21st century so far, a great share of prior understanding related to diverse fields such as personal symbolic <a href="/wiki/Name" title="Name">name</a> use, <a href="/wiki/Emotion_in_animals" title="Emotion in animals">animal emotions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal_culture" title="Animal culture">animal culture</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_learning" title="Animal learning">learning</a>, and even <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_sexuality" title="Animal sexuality">sexual conduct</a>, long thought to be well understood, has been revolutionized.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants_and_fungi">Plants and fungi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Plants and fungi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Communication is observed within the plant organism, i.e. within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_cells" title="Plant cells">plant cells</a> and between plant cells, between plants of the same or related species, and between plants and non-plant organisms, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Rhizosphere" title="Rhizosphere">root zone</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_root" title="Plant root">Plant roots</a> communicate with <a href="/wiki/Rhizome" title="Rhizome">rhizome</a> <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, and insects within the <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>. Recent research has shown that most of the microorganism plant communication processes are <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neuron</a>-like.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Plants also communicate via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volatile_oil" title="Volatile oil">volatiles</a> when exposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbivory" title="Herbivory">herbivory</a> attack behavior, thus warning neighboring plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In parallel they produce other volatiles to attract <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasites</a> which attack these herbivores.
</p><p>Fungi communicate to coordinate and organize their growth and development such as the formation of <a href="/wiki/Mycelium" title="Mycelium">mycelia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fruiting_body" title="Fruiting body">fruiting bodies</a>. Fungi communicate with their own and related species as well as with non fungal organisms in a great variety of symbiotic interactions, especially with bacteria, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicellular" title="Unicellular">unicellular</a> eukaryote, plants and insects through biochemicals of biotic origin. The biochemicals trigger the fungal organism to react in a specific manner, while if the same chemical molecules are not part of biotic messages, they do not trigger the fungal organism to react. This implies that fungal organisms can differentiate between molecules taking part in biotic messages and similar molecules being irrelevant in the situation. So far five different primary signalling molecules are known to coordinate different behavioral patterns such as <a href="/wiki/Filamentation" title="Filamentation">filamentation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mating" title="Mating">mating</a>, growth, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathogenicity" title="Pathogenicity">pathogenicity</a>. Behavioral coordination and production of signaling substances is achieved through interpretation processes that enables the organism to differ between self or non-self, a biotic indicator, biotic message from similar, related, or non-related species, and even filter out "noise", i.e. similar molecules without biotic content.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">Pheromones</a> are molecules released by one organism into the external environment to influence other individuals of the same species. Thus pheromone release is a form of communication. Pheromones promote sexual interaction (mating) in several fungal species. These include the aquatic fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Allomyces_macrogynus" title="Allomyces macrogynus">Allomyces macrogynus</a></i>, the Mucorales fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Mucor_mucedo" title="Mucor mucedo">Mucor mucedo</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Neurospora_crassa" title="Neurospora crassa">Neurospora crassa</a></i> and the yeasts <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Schizosaccharomyces_pombe" title="Schizosaccharomyces pombe">Schizosaccharomyces pombe</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rhodosporidium_toruloides" title="Rhodosporidium toruloides">Rhodosporidium toruloides</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacteria_quorum_sensing">Bacteria quorum sensing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Bacteria quorum sensing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Communication is not a tool used only by humans, plants and animals, but it is also used by microorganisms like bacteria. The process is called <a href="/wiki/Quorum_sensing" title="Quorum sensing">quorum sensing</a>. Through quorum sensing, bacteria can sense the density of cells, and regulate gene expression accordingly. This can be seen in both gram positive and gram negative bacteria.
This was first observed by Fuqua <i>et al.</i> in marine microorganisms like <i><a href="/wiki/Vibrio_harveyi" title="Vibrio harveyi">V. harveyi</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrio_fischeri" title="Vibrio fischeri">V. fischeri</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Transformation_(genetics)" title="Transformation (genetics)">Natural bacterial transformation</a> involves the transfer of naked DNA from one bacterium to another through the surrounding medium, and can be regarded as a relatively simple form of sexual interaction. In several bacterial species transformation is promoted by the production of an extracellular factor, termed a competence factor, that when released into the surrounding medium induces a state of competence in neighboring cells. The state of competence is the ability to take up the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> released by another cell. Bacterial competence factors are similar to pheromones in multicellular organisms. Competence factors have been studied in <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_subtilis" title="Bacillus subtilis">Bacillus subtilis</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Streptococcus_pneumoniae" title="Streptococcus pneumoniae">Streptococcus pneumoniae</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_History_of_Communication">The History of Communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: The History of Communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some of the oldest forms of human communication began as making sounds, dancing, acting, drawing or using symbols. Making sounds such as "Grunting or guttural sounds at a low pitch or high pitch would indicate either social communication or be a warning sign." Body language was also used as communication at this time. (SaHistory).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sahistoryOldest_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-sahistoryOldest-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Communication between humans began as language, symbols, songs, art, and dance. During this time cave drawings and murals that people painted on the walls told the story of their culture. "They would tell stories of battles, hunts and culture" <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sahistoryOldest_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-sahistoryOldest-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p><br/>
</p>
